      EMMA-500: E NHANCING M ASSIVELY M ULTILIN -
002   GUAL A DAPTATION OF L ARGE L ANGUAGE M ODELS

009                                              A BSTRACT
010
011            In this work, we introduce EMMA-500, a large-scale multilingual language model
012            continue-trained on texts across 546 languages designed for enhanced multilin-
013            gual performance, with a focus on improving language coverage for low-resource
014            languages. To facilitate continual pre-training, we compile the MaLA corpus, a
015            comprehensive multilingual dataset and enrich it with curated datasets across di-
016            verse domains. Leveraging this corpus, we conduct extensive continual pre-training
017            of the Llama 2 7B model, resulting in EMMA-500, which demonstrates robust per-
018
               formance across a wide collection of benchmarks, including a comprehensive set of
               multilingual tasks and PolyWrite, an open-ended generation benchmark developed
019
               in this study. Our results highlight the effectiveness of continual pre-training in
020
               expanding large language models’ language capacity, particularly for underrepre-
021            sented languages, demonstrating significant gains in cross-lingual transfer, task
022            generalization, and language adaptability.
023
024
025   1   I NTRODUCTION
026
027   Multilingual language models (MLMs) are designed to process and generate text in multiple languages.
028   These models have evolved rapidly over the past decade, fueled by advances in deep learning, e.g.,
029   Transformer networks (Vaswani et al., 2017), pre-training techniques, and the availability of large-
030   scale multilingual corpora such as mC4 (Raffel et al., 2020) and ROOTS (Laurençon et al., 2022). The
031   development of models like BERT (Devlin et al., 2019), GPT, and T5 (Raffel et al., 2020) opened the
032   door for multilingual counterparts such as mBERT, XLM-R (Conneau et al., 2020), mGPT (Shliazhko
033
      et al., 2022), and mT5 (Xue et al., 2021). These models were trained on massive multilingual corpora,
      allowing text in dozens of languages to be processed with the same set of model weights. Multilingual
034
      language models have shown impressive performance across various tasks like text classification
035
      and machine translation by leveraging cross-lingual transfer from high-resource languages such as
036   English and Chinese. However, many low-resource languages, with limited available data, remain
037   underrepresented. While large corpora are abundant for high-resource languages like English, French,
038   and Spanish, languages like Xhosa and Inuktitut suffer from scarce or fragmented data, leading to
039   imbalanced training and models that prioritize high-resource languages.
040
      Recent studies adopt continual pre-training to enhance the language coverage of large language models
041
      on low-resource languages. For example, Glot500 (Imani et al., 2023) and MaLA-500 (Lin et al.,
042   2024) use continual pre-training and vocabulary extension using XLM-R and LLaMA, respectively, on
043   the Glot500-c corpus covering 534 languages. xLLMs-100 (Lai et al., 2024) proceeds to multilingual
044   instruction fine-tuning to improve the multilingual performance of LLaMA and BLOOM models
045   on 100 languages, and Aya model (Üstün et al., 2024) applies continual training to the mT5 model
046   (Xue et al., 2021) using their constructed instruction dataset. LLaMAX (Lu et al., 2024) pushes the
047   envelope by focusing on translation tasks via continual pre-training of LLaMA in over 100 languages.
048   As the field of MLMs evolves, the role of data becomes increasingly critical in enhancing the
049   performance of the models, particularly when it comes to low-resource languages. To address this
050   need for more and better data, we extend existing work, such as MaLA-500, by expanding the corpus
051   for continual pre-training, coupled with large-scale training methods. We emphasize the creation of
052   a massively multilingual corpus that not only increases the quantity of data but also diversifies the
053   types of texts (e.g., code, books, scientific papers, and instructions). This ensures better language
      adaptation and broader language coverage, thus improving the representation and performance of


                                                       1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      multilingual language models, especially for underrepresented languages, ultimately creating more
055   inclusive and versatile language models that cater to a broader linguistic diversity. Our contribution is
056   summarized as follows: (1) We compile a massively multilingual corpus named MaLA to facilitate
057   continual training of large language models for enhanced language adaptation across a wide range
058   of linguistic contexts. (2) We extend the MaLA corpus by integrating multiple curated datasets,
059   creating a comprehensive and diverse data mix specifically for continual pre-training. (3) We perform
060   continual pre-training with the Llama 2 7B model1 on the multilingual corpus with 546 languages,
061   resulting in the new EMMA-500 model. This model is rigorously evaluated on a diverse set of tasks
062   and benchmarks, including PolyWrite, a novel open-ended generation benchmark developed as part
063
      of this work.
064
065
      The MaLA Corpus Our multilingual corpus features the following characteristics:
066
             • It contains 939 languages, 546 of which have more than 100k tokens and are used for
067
               training our EMMA-500 model, and 74 billion (B) whitespace delimited tokens in total.
068
069          • It has more than 300 languages with over 1 million whitespace delimited tokens and 546
070
               languages with over 100k tokens.
071          • It comes with four publicly available versions: (1) a noisy version after only basic pre-
072            processing like extraction and harmonization; (2) a cleaned version after data cleaning; (3)
073            a deduplicated version after approximate and exact deduplication; (4) a split version with
074
               train and valid splits.
075          • Our augmentation to the MaLA corpus includes different types of texts such as code, books,
076            scientific papers and instruction data, leading to a data mix with 100B+ whitespace delimited
077            tokens.
078
079   Evaluation Results In comparison to other decoder-only LLMs with parameter sizes from 4.5B to
080
      13B, including Llama 2-based multilingual models and the latest advanced models, our EMMA-500
      7B parameter model demonstrates strong performance. It achieves the lowest negative log-likelihood
081
      in intrinsic evaluation and significantly improves commonsense reasoning, machine translation, and
082
      open-ended generation tasks. It also outperforms all Llama 2-based models and multilingual LLMs in
083   text classification and natural language inference. While math and machine reading comprehension
084   remain challenging, our model still enhances the Llama 2 base model, and it surpasses the base model
085   in code generation without regression in performance.
086
087
088
      2     T HE M A LA C ORPUS
089
      The MaLA corpus—MaLA standing for Massive Language Adaptation—is a diverse and extensive
090
      compilation of text data encompassing 939 languages sourced from a wide array of datasets. It is
091
      developed for continual training of multilingual large language models. The source datasets the
092   MaLA corpus is compiled from exhibit a wide range of variance in various aspects. Examples of such
093   elements include the data quality, the nature of the text content, how the data sources were organized
094   into directory and file tree structures (if distributed as files rather than through an API) and the naming
095   conventions used therein, the data formats and structures in the files or in-memory objects containing
096   the text data, and the logic by which multilingual texts were aligned. This section introduces the
097   efforts made in data extraction, harmonization, pre-processing, cleaning, and deduplication in order
098   to build the corpus.
099
100   2.1   DATA P RE - PROCESSING
101
102
      To develop the MaLA corpus for training our language model, we establish a processing workflow
      consisting of the following key steps: (1) loading and curating identified data sources, (2) extracting
103
      and harmonizing both textual and metadata from these diverse sources into a unified format—often
104
      with tailored filtering, and (3) performing deduplication and further filtering on the textual data. In
105   step (1), source data are either organized and loaded into memory from a file tree structure or, if
106
         1
107        Choosing Llama 2 allows us to compare our model with many other models derived from it using continual
      pre-training. We plan to continue training models based on Llama 3/3.1 in the future.


                                                          2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      available, accessed through an API. In step (2), the output is designed to be JSON Lines (JSONL)
109   files with extracted text data and other desired content. A JSONL file contains multiple JSON records
110   for storing data, each separated by a newline character. We selectively process only data annotated as
111   training or development (validation) data, deliberately excluding test data.
112
113
      2.1.1     E XTRACTION AND H ARMONIZATION
114
115   As mentioned, there is significant variability in the data quality of the source datasets used for
116   compiling the corpus. Many of the datasets exhibit data quality challenges that would have adverse
117   effects on model training if left unaddressed. These dataset-specific challenges are typically addressed
118   during the pre-processing stage. For example, we identify one issue involving text records consisting
119   solely of date and timestamp information in the dataset for Languages of Russia (Corpora and Tools,
120   n.d.), likely resulting from a web scraper failing to differentiate between these elements and actual
121
      text. We address this by implementing a logic in the pre-processing script to detect and exclude such
      records. This issue is resolved by introducing a rule to identify and discard text containing consecutive
122
      repeating words. Due to the extensive volume of data, exhaustive examination of every source for
123
      data quality issues is impractical. Instead, we address issues only as we encounter them in our data
124   exploration and pre-processing pipeline development efforts. This approach likely leaves some data
125   quality problems undetected, since we do not go through the data systematically. Despite the need
126   for customized handling of certain dataset-specific idiosyncrasies, the core logic and structure of
127   the pre-processing workflow remain consistent across most datasets. We develop a standardized
128   pre-processing script that can be adapted with minor adjustments to accommodate different datasets.
129
130   2.1.2     L ANGUAGE C ODE N ORMALIZATION
131
132   An essential component of our pre-processing pipeline involved converting language codes to the
133   ISO 639-3 standard. This is crucial for ensuring consistent language identification across the source
134   datasets. We rely on the declared language of each dataset and normalize it to the ISO standard without
135
      performing additional language identification at this step. This approach helps maintain uniformity
      while streamlining the pre-processing workflow. We primarily use the PyPI library iso639-lang2
136
      or langcodes3 . While converting language codes to ISO 639-3, we encounter several challenges.
137
      One issue is that some languages in ISO 639-3 are divided into multiple subvarieties, but our source
138   data does not specify which subvariety is present. Our solution is to retain the original language code
139   from the dataset, even when it does not conform to the ISO 639-3 standard. Another issue arises
140   when certain languages are merged into other languages in the ISO 639-3 standard. In these cases,
141   we update the language code to reflect the merged language. Additionally, some language names or
142   codes in the source data—referred to as “original language names” or “original language codes”—are
143   not recognized by the conversion libraries. In some cases, the reason behind this is that the original
144   code in fact represents language families or groups of dialects (e.g., the ISO 639-2 codes “ber” for
145   Berber languages and “bih” for Bihari languages), rather than specific languages. If so, we then retain
146
      the original codes, despite their non-compliance with ISO 639-3. In other cases, the original language
      names are spelled differently from the standard recognized by the libraries. To address this mismatch,
147
      we implement a logic to detect and correct misspelled language names during pre-processing. All
148
      these “corner cases” require careful attention in the pre-processing stage to ensure correct language
149   code identification.
150
151
      2.1.3     W RITING S YSTEM R ECOGNITION
152
153   In addition to normalizing language codes, we also identify the script or writing system used in the
154   text data. We use the GlotScript library (Kargaran et al., 2024) to recognize writing systems
155   accordant to the ISO 15924 standard. The process begins by sampling 100 random lines from each
156   dataset (or the full dataset if it contains fewer than 100 lines). If GlotScript fails to identify a
157   script from this sample, we attempt identification using just the first line of the sample. If this still
158   does not yield a result, we set the script as “None”. It is worth noting that we choose not to classify a
159   dataset into multiple scripts, even when code-mixing (i.e., the use of multiple scripts) is present.
160
         2
161          https://pypi.org/project/iso639-lang/
         3
             https://pypi.org/project/langcodes


                                                         3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      If script identification is unsuccessful after the initial steps, we assume the script matched a previously
163   detected one for that language. In cases where no previous script information exists, we refer to a
164   mapping of languages and their default scripts provided by the Glot500 corpora collection. Through
165   this multi-step process, we are able to determine the script for every dataset without exceptions.
166
      During script identification, we encounter several challenges. One issue is determining an appropriate
167
      length for the text chunk used for script recognition. A chunk that is too short could lead to incorrect
168
      identification if the text contains quotes or foreign language fragments using a different writing system.
169   Conversely, using a chunk that is too long could result in excessive resource usage, slowing down
170   processing or even causing memory exhaustion. Another consideration is whether to assume that a
171   single file or dataset might contain multiple scripts. Such an assumption would require identifying
172   the script at a more granular level, such as paragraph by paragraph or even sentence by sentence.
173   Alternatively, we could assume that each file or dataset contained only one “main” script. This
174   assumption would allow us to identify the script from a representative sample of the text for the whole
175   file or dataset. We adopt the latter approach, recognizing a single dominant script for each dataset.
176   The output of this process is a label in the format language Script, e.g., eng Latn, where “Language”
177
      represents the ISO 639-3 language code and “Script” represents the ISO 15924 script code.
178
179   2.2     DATA C LEANING
180
      Most source data has already undergone data cleaning to different extents. Nonetheless, different
181
      cleaning processes have been adopted. We continue to clean the data to ensure consistency and
182   accuracy for monolingual and bilingual texts. Following the pipeline used by BigScience’s pipeline
183   for ROOTS corpus (Laurençon et al., 2022), we further adopt some necessary data cleaning to
184   filter out text samples that might have undesirable quality. We first perform document modification
185   for monolingual texts. The first step is whitespace standardization: all types of whitespace in a
186   document are converted into a single, consistent space character. We split documents by newline
187   characters, tabs, and spaces, strip words, and reconstruct the documents to remove very long words.
188   However, these two steps do not apply to languages without whitespace word delimiters like Chinese,
189   Japanese, Korean, Thai, Lao, Burmese, etc. We also remove words containing certain patterns, e.g.,
190
      “http” and “.com”, which are likely to be links and page source code. We then perform document
      filtering, including word count filtering, character repetition filtering, word repetition filtering, special
191
      characters filtering, stop words filtering, and flag words filtering. We re-identify the languages that are
192
      supported by the pre-trained fastText-based language identification model (Joulin et al., 2016b;a).
193   For other languages, we assume the language identification of the original data source and language
194   code conversion are reliable.
195
196
      As we collect data from different sources, we deduplicate the data to remove the overlap between
      different sources using MinHash and exact deduplication (Mou et al., 2023), with details described in
197
      Appendix A.3.
198
199
      2.3     K EY S TATISTICS
200
201   This section presents the final MaLA corpus obtained after data sourcing, pre-processing, cleaning,
202   and deduplication. Table 1 first shows some basic data statistics and compares them with other
203   multilingual corpora for pre-training language models or language adaptation. Additional statistics
204   are presented in Appendix B in the appendix. The MaLA corpus harvests a wide range of datasets in
205   multiple domains. Table 7 in the appendix lists the corpora and collections we used as monolingual
206   data sources. The token counts are based on white-space delimitation, though it might not be accurate
207   for languages like Chinese, Japanese and Korean since the entire clause is counted as one token.
208   Glot500-c (Imani et al., 2023) has 534 languages in total, in which 454 languages are directly
209
      distributed on Huggingface 4 . We also omit high-resource languages in the other three datasets,
      i.e., MADLAD (Kudugunta et al., 2024), CulturaX (Nguyen et al., 2023), and CC100 (Wenzek
210
      et al., 2020), as our main focus is continual pre-training for language adaptation. The final MaLA
211
      corpus consists of 939 languages, 546 of which have more than 100k tokens and are used for training
212   our EMMA-500 model. Counting languages with more than 1 million tokens, the MaLA corpus
213   and Glot500-c have more than 300, while MADLAD and CulturaX have 200 and 100 respectively.
214   Compared with Glot500-c, the MaLA corpus contains documents with significantly higher sequence
215

             https://huggingface.co/datasets/cis-lmu/Glot500

224
225   lengths, with an average token count of 90 versus 19. This higher sequence length is advantageous for
226   continually training LLMs because it provides more context within each training example, allowing
227   the model to better capture long-range dependencies and patterns in the data. As a result, MaLA is
228   more effective for language adaptation.
229
230
      3       DATA M IXING AND M ODEL T RAINING
231
232
      Incorporating a diverse data mix—spanning various languages, domains, document lengths and
233   styles—is crucial for continual training of large language models to enhance their versatility, gen-
234   eralization ability, and robustness across a wide range of tasks and domains. We augment the
235   MaLA corpus with diverse data to mitigate issues such as over-fitting to specific styles or topics or
236   underperforming on tasks outside the training distribution.
237
238   3.1     DATA M IXING
239
240   Curated Data We enhance the corpus with high-quality curated data, specifically high-resource
241   languages in the monolingual part. We use texts from scientific papers as these provide a structured,
242   information-dense corpus that can improve the model’s ability to handle technical language and
243   domain-specific content. They are (1) CSL (Li et al., 2022), a large-scale Chinese Scientific Liter-
244
      ature dataset, that contains titles, abstracts, keywords and academic fields of 396,209 papers; (2)
      pes2o (Soldaini & Lo, 2023), a collection of full-text open-access academic papers derived from the
245
      Semantic Scholar Open Research Corpus (S2ORC) (Lo et al., 2020). We further add free e-books
246
      from the Gutenberg project5 compiled by Faysse (2023). These texts enhance the range of literary
247   styles and narrative forms, thus enhancing the model’s versatility. Adding high-resource languages
248   into the pre-training corpora also mitigates the forgetting in model training.
249
250   Instruction Data We further augmented the training corpus by incorporating instruction-based
251   datasets, inspired by Li et al. (2023); Taylor et al. (2022); Nakamura et al. (2024). We mix two
252   instruction data into our training corpus. They are: (1) xp3x (Crosslingual Public Pool of Prompts
253   eXtended)6 , a multitask instruction collection in 277 languages (Muennighoff et al., 2022); (2)
254   the Aya collection 7 that contains both human-curated and machine translated instructions in 101
255   languages (Singh et al., 2024). For both instruction datasets, we use their training set.
256
257   Code We additionally enrich the training corpus by sourcing code data from The Stack (Kocetkov
258   et al., 2023). This is done following existing work that demonstrates the value of code data in
259
      improving the reasoning ability of language models (Zhang et al., 2024b; Ma et al., 2024) while also
      mitigating any catastrophic forgetting of the base model’s programming knowledge.
260
261   We subsample The Stack at an effective rate of 15.2%, prioritizing high-quality source files and data
262   science code 8 . We retain the 32 most important non-data programming languages by prevalence
263   while also adding in all llvm code following prior work detailing its importance in multi-lingual
264   code generation (Szafraniec et al., 2023; Paul et al., 2024). We also source from data-heavy formats
265       5
          https://www.gutenberg.org/
266       6
          https://huggingface.co/datasets/CohereForAI/xP3x
267     7
          https://huggingface.co/datasets/CohereForAI/aya_collection_language_
268   split

287   but follow precedent (Lozhkov et al., 2024) and subsample them more aggressively. For a more
288   detailed read on filtering heuristics, we direct the reader to Appendix A.2.
289
290   Data Mix Our final data mix for continual training is listed in Table 2. The resource categorization
291   refers to Appendix B.1 in the appendix and inst medium-high+ is a separate category with
292   languages with more than 500 million but less than 1B tokens. For monolingual text, we also have
293   a seperate category for English. In continual learning, where new data is introduced to an existing
294   model, there is a risk of “catastrophic forgetting”, where the model loses knowledge from earlier
295
      training stages. Although our work’s primary focus is in a low-resource regime, we enhance the
      training corpus with a wide range of data types, including books and scientific papers in high-resource
296
      languages, code, and instruction data in our data mix. We downsample texts in high-resource
297
      languages and upsample text in low-resource languages using different sample rates according to
298   how resourceful the language is. We make our data mix diverse and balanced towards different
299   resource groups of languages in order to retain the prior knowledge of the model while learning new
300   information, especially in medium- and low-resource languages, thus maintaining high performance
301   across both previously seen and new languages. The final data mix has around 136B tokens.
302
303   3.2      M ODEL T RAINING
304
305   We employ continual training using the causal language modelling objective for the decoder-only
306   Llama model and exposing the pre-trained model to new data to develop our EMMA-500 model.
307   We adopt efficient training strategies combining optimization, memory management, precision
308
      handling, and distributed training techniques. Our EMMA-500 model is trained on the Leonardo
      supercomputer9 , occupying 256 Nvidia A100 GPUs, using the GPT-NeoX framework (Andonian
309
      et al., 2023). During training, we set a global batch size of 4096 and worked with sequences of 4096
310
      tokens. The training process ran for 12,000 steps, resulting in a total of 200 billion Llama 2 tokens.
311   We use the Adam optimizer (Kingma & Ba, 2015) with a learning rate of 0.0001, betas of [0.9, 0.95],
312   and an epsilon of 1e-8. We use a cosine learning rate scheduler with a warm-up of 500 iterations. To
313   reduce memory consumption, activation checkpointing is employed. Precision is managed through
314   mixed-precision techniques, using bfloat16 for computational efficiency and maintaining FP32 for
315   gradient accumulation.
316
317

345   performance on downstream tasks. Table 3 lists the datasets we used as downstream evaluation
346
      datasets in this work.
347   We present the evaluation results of intrinsic evaluation in Appendix E.1, commonsense reasoning in
348   Appendix E.3, text summarization in Appendix E.2, math tasks in Appendix E.6, machine reading
349   comprehension in Appendix E.7, and coding generation in Appendix E.8.
350
351   Baselines We compare our model with three groups of decoder-only models. They are (1) Llama 2
352   models (Touvron et al., 2023) and continual pre-trained models based on Llama 2, such as CodeL-
353   lama (Roziere et al., 2023), MaLA-500 (Lin et al., 2024), LLaMAX (Lu et al., 2024), Tower (Alves
354
      et al., 2024), and YaYi10 ; (2) other LLMs and continual pre-trained LLMs designed to be massively
      multilingual, including BLOOM (Scao et al., 2022), mGPT (Shliazhko et al., 2022), XGLM (Lin
355
      et al., 2022), and Occiglot11 ; and (3) recent LLMs with superior English capabilities like Llama
356
      3 (Dubey et al., 2024), Llama 3.112 , Qwen 2 (Yang et al., 2024a), and Gemma 2 (Team et al., 2024).
357   There are also some other LLMs such as OpenAI’s API models 13 and xLLMs-100 (Lai et al., 2024).
358   However, they do not release the model weights or they limit access to them through commercial API,
359   so we did not include them. The MADLAD model (Kudugunta et al., 2024) that uses the decoder-only
360   T5 architecture is not supported by inference engines such as the HuggingFace transformers (Wolf
361   et al., 2019). We do not compare them in this work.
362

365   FLORES-200 is an evaluation benchmark for translation tasks with 204 language pairs involving

      assess all language models by adopting a 3-shot evaluation approach with the prompt in Appendix D.1.
367

370   applied to the texts and chrF++ uses word order 2. The choice of flores200 tokenization ensures
371   that languages that do not have a whitespace delimiter can be evaluated at the (sub-)word level. For

      reproducibility, we attach the BLEU and chrF++ signatures.14,15

400   Table 4 presents the average X-to-English (X-Eng) translation results.16 Our EMMA-500 model
401   outperforms all other models on average. We achieve the best performance across all language
402
      settings, except for high-resource languages where our model slightly lags behind Llama 3/3.1,
      Gemma 7B, and LlaMAX 7B Alpaca. In the English-to-X (Eng-X) translation direction, as shown in
403
      Table 16 in Appendix E.4, the advantage of EMMA-500 is even more pronounced. We outperform
404
      all other models even in high-resource languages, and the advantage becomes more significant in
405   lower-resource languages. Overall, we note that our model outperforms Tower models which are
406   explicitly adjusted to perform translation tasks in high-resource languages. Further, the much larger
407   margin between EMMA-500 and other models in Eng-X compared with X-Eng indicates that our
408   EMMA-500 model is particularly good at generating non-English texts.
409
410   4.3   T EXT C LASSIFICATION
411
412   SIB-200 (Adelani et al., 2023) and Taxi1500 (Ma et al., 2023) are two prominent topic classification
413   datasets. SIB-200 encompasses seven categories: science/technology, travel, politics, sports, health,
414   entertainment, and geography. Taxi1500 spans 1507 languages, involving six classes: Recommen-
415
      dation, Faith, Description, Sin, Grace, and Violence. We use 3-shot prompting with prompts in
      Appendix D.2, drawing demonstrations from the development set and testing models on the test split.
416
      The outcomes on SIB-200 and TAXI-1500 are tabulated in Table 5. For SIB-200, our EMMA-500
417
      model outperforms all Llama2-based models, with particularly notable gains in languages with
418   medium or fewer resources—seeing an average improvement of 47.5%. Taxi-1500 could be a more
419   challenging task since it is in the religious domain, but our model still surpasses all Llama2-based
420   models except for MalA-500. However, despite these improvements in both classification tasks, our
421   models lag behind the latest models such as Llama3 and 3.1, especially in high-resource languages.
422

454
455
456
      2018). BLEU is crucial for assessing the linguistic accuracy and relevance of the generated text in
457
      comparison to the expected human-like text present in the dataset. Self-BLEU is used to evaluate the
458
      diversity of the text generated by a model. It measures how similar different texts from the same
459   model are to each other by treating one generated text as the “candidate” and others as the “reference”
460   texts. This metric is useful in scenarios where high degrees of variation are desirable, as it helps
461   identify models that might be overfitting to particular styles or patterns of text.
462
      The results are presented in Table 6. The BLEU metric is an indicator that measures how generated
463
      texts are close to the references. However, in open-ended generation, LLMs cannot generate identical
464
      texts to references, leading to low BLEU scores. The EMMA-500 model obtains remarkably high
465   BLEU scores in both high-resource and low-resource settings when compared with baselines. Its
466   performance in low-resource settings is particularly noteworthy, as it not only sustains high BLEU
467   scores but also exhibits a Self-BLEU score of 5.09, the highest among all models evaluated. This
468   high Self-BLEU score indicates less diversity in the generated text, suggesting that while EMMA-500
469   maintains consistency, it may produce less varied outputs. When compared to other high-performing
470   models like Qwen 2 7B and Llama 3.1 8B, the EMMA-500 model exhibits a superior balance between
471   accuracy and linguistic creativity. Unlike Qwen 2 7B, which shows a spike in performance primarily
472   in medium-low resource settings, EMMA-500 maintains a consistently high performance across
473
      varying levels of resource availability.
474
475   PolyWrite This is a novel multilingual benchmark composed in this work for evaluating open-
476   ended generation in 240 languages. We use ChatGPT to generate different prompts in English and use
477   Google Translate to translate them into different languages for models to generate creative content.
478   This benchmark consists of 31 writing tasks, such as storytelling and email writing, and 155 prompts
479   in total. We back-translate the multilingual prompts to English, calculate the BLEU scores between
480   original English prompts and back-translation, and filter out translated prompts with BLEU scores
481   below 20, and the entire dataset contains a total of 35,751 prompts. The details of PolyWrite are
482
      described in Appendix C.1.
483   We use Self-BLEU (Zhu et al., 2018) to evaluate the diversity of generated texts in the PolyWrite
484   benchmark, as presented in Table 6. A lower Self-BLEU score indicates more diverse generation,
485   but does not means a better generation quality. Our EMMA-500 model demonstrates comparable
      performance across various languages. Compared to other models like Llama 3/3.1 and Qwen 1.5/2,


                                                                             9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
      particularly in medium-low and low-resource languages, EMMA-500 has higher Self-BLEU scores,
487   indicating lower diversity in its generated content.
488
489   Evaluating open-ended generation poses significant challenges, as it goes beyond simply measuring
490
      accuracy or correctness. Metrics like BLEU or Self-BLEU, while useful for assessing similarity to
      reference texts or the diversity of given texts, often fail to capture more nuanced aspects. Subjective
491
      factors like cultural relevance and the appropriateness of responses in low-resource languages are
492
      difficult to quantify. This makes it challenging to create evaluation benchmarks and metrics that fully
493   capture the strengths and weaknesses of models like EMMA-500 in diverse, real-world scenarios.
494

517
518
519
520   5     C ONCLUSION AND O UTLOOKS
521
522
523   This paper addresses critical advancements and challenges in adapting language models to more than
524   500 languages, focusing on enhancing their performance across diverse languages. We compile the
525
      MaLA corpus, a multilingual dataset for continual pre-training of multilingual language models. By
      expanding and augmenting existing corpora, we train the EMMA-500 model. It demonstrates notable
526
      improvements in a range of tasks such as next-token prediction, commonsense reasoning, machine
527
      translation, text classification, and open-ended generation, showing remarkable improvements in low-
528   resource languages. Our results show that a well-curated, massively multilingual corpora can advance
529   model capabilities. This work sets a new benchmark for inclusive and effective multilingual language
530   models and paves the way for future research to address the disparities between high-resource and
531   low-resource languages.
532
      Multilingual language models are designed to cater to diverse linguistic and cultural backgrounds, yet
533
      many existing multilingual benchmarks rely on human or machine translations, primarily reflecting
534   English-speaking communities and introducing imperfections that affect evaluation integrity. We
535   emphasize the need for natively-created multilingual test sets to provide more accurate assessments.
536   While our EMMA-500 model shows enhanced multilingual performance compared to Llama 2 7B
537   and other variants, it falls short on certain benchmarks compared to newer models like Llama 3 and
538   Gemma 2. Future efforts will explore multilingual extension with these models and direct pre-training
539   using the released corpus, along with multilingual instruction tuning to improve task performance
      and interactions.

       A     DATA S OURCES
1124
1125
       A.1   M ONOLINGUAL DATA
1126
1127   Table 7 lists the corpora and collections we use as monolingual data sources in this work. Monolingual
1128   data sources simply contain text data in a single language.
1129
1130   Metadata For the monolingual data sources, we define the contents of the JSONL output file
1131   from the pre-processing workflow to consist of the fields url, text, collection, source, and
1132   original code. The contents of these fields are as follows. The field text contains the language
1133   data in a granularity specific to the given corpus. If the granularity was sentence-level, then we could
       expect the sentences in the corpus to generally be independent of each other, while only parts of the


                                                         21
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1134
       sentences—such as phrases, clauses, and words—exhibit serial dependence. If the granularity was
1135   paragraph-level, then we could expect the sentences within paragraphs to have serial dependence,
1136   while paragraphs to largely be independent of each other. The field url contains a URL indicating
1137   the web address from which the text data has been extracted, if available. The field collection
1138   contains the name of the collection, i.e., a corpus or a set of corpora, which the text is extracted from,
1139   whereas the field source contains the name of a more specific part of the collection, such as the
1140   name of an individual corpus or a file in the collection the text was extracted from. Lastly, the field
1141   original code contains the language code of the text data as it is designated in the data source,
1142   e.g., in the directory structure, the filenames, or the data object returned by an API call.
1143

1207   A.2    C ODE
1208
1209   All the programming language splits are filtered using the following conditions:
1210
              • For files forked more than 25 times, we retain them if the average line length is less than
1211
                120, the maximum line length is less than 300, and the alphanumeric fraction is more than
1212            30%.
1213
              • For files forked between 15 and 25 times, we retain them if the average line length is less
1214
                than 90, the maximum line length is less than 150, and the alphanumeric fraction is more
1215
                than 40%.
1216
1217
              • For files forked less than 15 times, we retain them if the average line length is less than 80,
                the maximum line length is less than 120, and the alphanumeric fraction is more than 45%.
1218
1219   Subsequently, an aggressive MinHash deduplication pipeline with a threshold of 0.5 and a shingle
1220   size of 20 is applied. Finally, the resultant language splits are then capped at 5 million samples each.
1221

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1242
       MinHash Deduplication For each language’s dataset in each writing system, we start by using the
1243   MinHashLSH algorithm (Rajaraman & Ullman, 2011) to filter out similar documents. It is a near-
1244   deduplication technique that builds on MinHash (Broder, 1997), utilizing multiple hash functions for
1245   n-grams and the Jaccard similarity, and incorporates Locality-Sensitive Hashing to enhance efficiency.
1246   We use the implementation by text-dedup repository (Mou et al., 2023), applying 5-grams and a
1247   similarity threshold of 0.7 to identify similar documents based on the Jaccard index.
1248
1249   Exact Deduplication We further deploy exact deduplication using the text-dedup repository
1250   again with precise matching. It takes each document through a hash function, i.e., MD5 (Rivest,
1251   1992) in our choice, and the hash values of all documents are compared to identify duplicates.
1252
1253
1254
       B     A DDITIONAL S TATISTICS OF M A LA C ORPUS
1255
1256   B.1   S UPPORTED L ANGUAGES
1257
       Table 8 shows the languages codes of MaLA corpus, where “unseen” means the languages are not used
1258
       for training EMMA-500. The classification system for token counts categorizes language resources
1259
       based on their size into five distinct tiers: “high” for resources exceeding 1 billion tokens, indicating
1260   a vast amount of data; “medium-high” for those with more than 100 million tokens, reflecting a
1261   substantial dataset; “medium” for resources that contain over 10 million tokens, representing a
1262   moderate size; “medium-low” for datasets with over “1 million tokens”, indicating a smaller yet
1263   significant amount of data; and finally, “low” for resources containing less than 1 million tokens,
1264   which suggests a minimal data presence. This hierarchy helps in understanding the scale and potential
1265   utility of the language resources available. Figure 1 shows the number of texts and tokens in different
1266   resource groups.
1267
1268   B.2   DATA A NALYSIS
1269
1270   We examine the Unicode block distribution of each language, which counts the percentage of tokens
1271   falling into the Unicode block of each language. This aims to check whether language code conversion
1272   and writing system recognition are reasonably good. Figure 2 shows the Unicode block distribution.
1273
       The result aligns with our observation of the presence of code-mixing, but in general, the majority of
       languages have tokens falling in their own Unicode blocks.
1274
1275   We also check the data source distribution as shown in Figure 3 to see where the texts in the MaLA
1276   corpus come from. The main source is common crawl, e.g., CC 2018, CC, OSCAR, and CC 20220801.
1277   A large number of documents come from Earthlings which comes from Glot500-c (Imani et al., 2023).
1278   For web-crawled data with a URL in the original metadata of corpora like CulturaX (Nguyen et al.,
1279
       2023) and HPLT (de Gibert et al., 2024), we extract the domain. Thus, the final corpus has many
       sources with a small portion.
1280
1281
1282   C     E VALUATION B ENCHMARKS
1283
1284   C.1   P OLY W RITE
1285
1286   The PolyWrite dataset has 51 writing tasks with the number of prompts per task shown in Figure 4.
1287   We use Google Translate to translate the English prompts into 240 languages and back-translate for
1288   translation quality assessment.
1289
       The metadata of PolyWrite includes several key fields. The category specifies the task type. The
1290   name field typically holds the specific identifier for each prompt, while prompt en contains the
1291   English version of the prompt. lang script identifies the language and script used, ensuring
1292   correct language processing. The prompt translated field holds the translated prompt in the
1293   target language, and prompt backtranslated contains the back-translated version to assess
1294   translation quality. Both bleu and chrf++ fields provide numeric evaluation metrics, with BLEU
1295   and chrF++ scores measuring the quality of the generated text. Finally, the uuid ensures a unique
       identifier for each dataset entry, allowing for precise reference and tracking of individual prompts. To

1490   E         D ETAILED E VALUATION
1491
1492   This section presents detailed results of the evaluation. For benchmarks consisting of multiple lan-
1493   guages, we host the results on GitHub40 . Those benchmarks include ARC multilingual, BELEBELE,
1494   Glot500-c test set, PBC, SIB-200, Taxi-1500, FLORES200, XLSum, Aya evaluation suite, and
1495   PolyWrite. For FLORES200, XLSum, Aya evaluation suite, and PolyWrite, we also release the
1496   generated texts of all compared models.
1497
1498   Evaluation      Software We          use     the    Language     Model     Evaluation      Harness
1499   (lm-evaluation-harness) framework (Gao et al., 2023) for benchmarking test sets
1500   that are already ingested in the framework. For other benchmarks, we use in-house developed
1501
       evaluation scripts and other open-source implementations. In text classification tasks such as
       SIB-200 and Taxi-1500, the evaluation protocol involves calculating the probability of the next
1502
       output token for each candidate category. These probabilities are then sorted in descending order,
1503
       with the category having the highest probability being selected as the model’s prediction. This
1504   process is implemented using the Transformers (Wolf et al., 2019) library. In tasks like Machine
1505   Translation and Open-Ended Generation, the vLLM library (Kwon et al., 2023) is used to accelerate
1506   inference, providing significant speed improvements. Similarly, for code generation tasks, we use a
1507   VLLM-enabled evaluation harness package (vllm-code-harness)41 for the execution-tested
1508   evaluations. For massively multilingual benchmarks with more than 100 languages, we categorize
1509   languages into five groups as listed in Table 8 in Appendix B.1.
1510
            40
1511             https://github.com/MaLA-LM/emma-500/tree/main/evaluation_results
            41
                 https://github.com/iNeil77/vllm-code-harness

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

1534
1535   E.1     I NTRINSIC E VALUATION
1536
1537   For intrinsic evaluation, we compute the negative log-likelihood (NLL) of the test text given by the
1538
       tested LLMs instead of using length-normalized perplexity due to different text tokenization schemes
       across models. We concatenate the test set as a single input and run a sliding-window approach.42
1539
       To make a comparison across models, we use the Glot500-c test set, which covers 534 evaluated
1540
       languages. We also test on the Parallel Bible Corpus (PBC) which could yield NLL more comparable
1541   across languages. Table 9 and Table 10 show the intrinsic evaluation results on Glot500-c test and
1542   PBC, respectively. As shown, our model attains lower NLL compared to all other models on both
1543   test sets and languages with different resource availability. This suggests that the test sets are more
1544   similar to the underlying training data of our model and it can be interpreted that EMMA-500 has
1545   learned to compress massive multilingual text more efficiently.
1546
1547   E.2     S UMMARIZATION
1548
1549   XL-Sum (Hasan et al., 2021) is a large-scale multilingual abstractive summarization dataset that
1550   covers 44 languages. To evaluate the quality of the generated summaries, we use ROUGE-L (Lin,
1551   2004) and BERTScore (Zhang et al., 2019) as evaluation metrics. ROUGE-L measures the longest
1552
       common subsequence (LCS) between the reference and generated summaries. Recall and precision
       are calculated by dividing the LCS length by the reference length and the generated summary length,
1553
       respectively. An F-score is then used to combine these two aspects into a single metric. BERTScore
1554
       computes the semantic similarity between summaries by leveraging contextual embeddings from
1555   pre-trained language models. Specifically, we use the bert-base-multilingual-cased43
1556   model for BERTScore to accommodate multiple languages.
1557
1558
       The evaluation results are presented in Table 11, we observe that our model, EMMA-500, performs
       comparably to other Llama2-based models like TowerInstruct Llama 2 7B. While EMMA-500 shows
1559
       strength in certain language categories, it does not significantly outperform these models overall. This
1560
       suggests that, although EMMA-500 is effective in multilingual summarization tasks, there is room
1561   for improvement to achieve more consistent performance across all languages. It is also important to
1562   note that this test set includes only 44 languages, limiting the evaluation of EMMA-500’s capabilities
1563   in low-resource languages.
1564
         42
1565          https://huggingface.co/docs/transformers/en/perplexity
         43
              https://huggingface.co/google-bert/bert-base-multilingual-cased

                                                                             30
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1620
       We assess the models’ commonsense reasoning ability using three multilingual benchmarks. XCOPA
1621   (Ponti et al., 2020) is a dataset covering 11 languages that focuses on causal commonsense rea-
1622   soning across multiple languages. XStoryCloze (Lin et al., 2022) is derived from the English
1623   StoryCloze dataset (Mostafazadeh et al., 2017) and translated into 10 non-English languages, testing
1624   commonsense reasoning within a story. In this task, the system must choose the correct ending
1625   for a four-sentence narrative. XWinograd (Tikhonov & Ryabinin, 2021) is a multilingual collec-
1626   tion of Winograd Schemas (Levesque et al., 2012) available in six languages, aimed at evaluating
1627   cross-lingual commonsense reasoning.
1628
1629   E.3    C OMMONSENSE R EASONING
1630
1631   We perform zero-shot evaluations. Accuracy is used as the evaluation metric. We categorize languages
1632
       into different resource groups based on language availability, accessibility, and possible corpus size.
       For XCOPA, we have three groups, i.e., high-resource (Italian, Turkish, Vietnamese, and Chinese),
1633
       medium-resource (Swahili due to its regional influence, Estonian, Haitian Creole, Indonesian, Thai,
1634
       and Tamil), and low-resource languages (Cusco-Collao Quechua).44 For XStoryCloze, the resource
1635   group is high-resource (Arabic, English, Spanish, Russian, and Chinese), medium (Hindi, Indonesian,
1636   Swahili, and Telugu), and low (Basque and Burmese). For XWinograd, there is only one group for
1637   high-resource languages. Table 12 shows the evaluation results of zero-shot commonsense reasoning.
1638
       Compared with Llama 2-based models on XCOPA, our model improves the average performance by
1639
       a large margin—up to a 5% increase when compared with the best-performing TowerInstruct based
1640
       on Llama 2 7B.45 Our model also outperforms all the multilingual LLMs. Recent LLMs such as
1641   Gemma and Llama 3 have stronger performance than Llama 2 models, and Gemma 2 9B performs the
1642   best. However, our model achieves better performance than Qwen, Llama 3, and 3.1. We gain similar
1643   results on XStoryCloze, outperforming all the models except Gemma 2 9B. As for XWinograd, a
1644   multilingual benchmark with high-resource only, our model achieves improved performance than
1645   Llama 2, despite not being as good as Tower models, which target high-resource languages. However,
1646   our model is comparable to other multilingual LLMs. For low-resource languages, our model
1647   outperforms all the compared LLMs except LLaMAX Llama 2 7B Alpaca on XCOPA, where we
1648   achieve the same accuracy as it.
1649
1650   E.5    NATURAL L ANGUAGE I NFERENCE
1651
1652   We evaluate on the XNLI (Cross-lingual Natural Language Inference) benchmark (Conneau et al.,
1653
       2018) where sentence pairs in different languages need to be classified as entailment, contradiction,
       or neutral. We categorize the languages in XNLI into 3 groups, i.e., high-resource (German, English,
1654
       Spanish, French, Russian, and Chinese), medium-resource (Arabic, Bulgarian, Greek, Hindi, Turkish,
1655
       and Vietnamese), and low-resource (Swahili, Thai, and Urdu).46 Table 17 shows the aggregated
1656   accuracy. Our model outperforms most baselines including Llama 2-based models and multilingual
1657   LLMs. We achieve the second-best average accuracy, slightly behind the Llama 3.1 8B model. On
1658   the low-resource end, we perform the second-best, slightly behind the Gemma 2 9B model.
1659
1660   E.6    M ATH
1661
1662   We evaluate the ability of LLMs to solve grade-school math problems across multiple languages on
1663   the MGSM (Multilingual Grade School Math) benchmark (Shi et al., 2022). MGSM is an extension
1664   of the GSM8K (Grade School Math 8K) dataset (Cobbe et al., 2021) by translating 250 of the original
1665   GSM8K problems into ten languages. We also split these ten languages into three groups, i.e.,
1666   high-resource (Spanish, French, German, Russian, Chinese, and Japanese), medium-resource (Thai,
1667
       Swahili, and Bengali), and low-resource (Telugu). Table 19 shows the results for 3-shot prompting
       with a flexible match to obtain the answers in model generation. We evaluate all the models by
1668
       directly prompting the questions (denoted as direct) and the questions with answers followed by
1669
       Chain-of-Thoughts prompt in the same languages as the subset being evaluated (denoted as CoT)
1670
1671     44
             Note that there is no perfect categorization for language resource groups.
         45
1672         The biggest improvements are on medium-resource languages. However, we note that the resource catego-
1673   rization is not perfect.

             Again, the categorization is not perfect.

1697
1698   (Shi et al., 2022). The results show that Llama 2 7B is a weak model in the MGSM math task. The
1699   base model and its variants failed in most settings. Multilingual LLMs such as BLOOM, XGLM, and
1700   YaYi are also subpar at this task. Recent LLMs like Llama 3, Qwen, and Gemma obtain reasonable
1701   performance, and Qwen series models are the best. Our model improves the Llama 2 7B model
1702   remarkably in both prompt strategies. For direct prompting, our model has an average accuracy of
1703   0.1702, 7% higher than the Llama 2 7B chat model. For CoT-based prompting, our model increases
1704
       the score of the Llama 2 7B chat model from 0.1 to 0.3 (20% higher) and slightly outperforms Llama
       3 and 3.1 8B models.
1705
1706
1707   E.7     M ACHINE R EADING C OMPREHENSION
1708
       BELEBELE (Bandarkar et al., 2023) is a machine reading comprehension dataset covering 122
1709
       languages including high- and low-resource languages. Each question offers four multiple-choice
1710
       answers based on a short passage from the FLORES-200 dataset. This benchmark is very challenging,
1711   even the English version of it presents remarkable challenges for advanced models. Table 22 shows
1712   the zero-shot results in different resource groups. Our continual pre-training improves the Llama 2
1713   7B base model. But Llama 2 7 B-based models mostly fail in this task and get quasi-random results.
1714   Recent advanced models like Llama 3.1 and Qwen 2 get reasonable results.
1715
       We then move to a more challenging task, the ARC multilingual test, which is a machine-translated
1716
       benchmark (Lai et al., 2023) from the ARC dataset (Clark et al., 2018) that contains English science
1717   exam questions for multiple grade levels. We test the five-shot performance, with results shown in
1718   Table 23. The evaluation results show a similar pattern to BELEBELE that EMMA-500 improves
1719   Llama 2 but the 7B model is not capable of this challenging task, all Llama2 7B-based models obtain
1720   close to random results, and recent advances like Llama 3, Gemma, and Qwen get much better results.
1721
1722   E.8     C ODE G ENERATION
1723
1724   We conduct code generation evaluations on the Multipl-E (Cassano et al., 2022) benchmark in the
1725   interest of measuring the effects of massively multilingual continual pre-training on a model’s code
1726   generation utility and detecting if any catastrophic forgetting (Luo et al., 2023) has occurred on
1727   this front. Importantly, this also has implications for a model’s reasoning (Yang et al., 2024b) and
       entity-tracking abilities (Kim et al., 2024).

1774
       We perform test-case-based execution-tested evaluations using the pass@k metric (Chen et al.,
1775
       2021). We benchmark for k values of 1,10, and 25 using a generation pool of 50 samples per
1776   problem. Table 24 outlines comparisons against strong, openly available multilingual models such
1777   as Qwen (Yang et al., 2024a) and Aya (Üstün et al., 2024) along with other continually pre-trained
1778   models. Our main takeaway is that by carefully curating high-quality code data as part of the data
1779   mix, it is possible to not only avoid the catastrophic forgetting that has plagued existing continually
1780   pre-trained models (MaLA-500, LLaMAX and TowerBase) but also surpass the base model itself.
1781   However, the pre-training phase is still the most important, and closing the gap with stronger base
       models like Qwen and Gemma remains elusive.

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

1831   Multilingual LLMs Multilingual large language models (LLMs) have made significant progress
1832   in processing and understanding multiple languages within a unified framework. Models like mT5
1833   (Xue et al., 2021) and XGLM (Lin et al., 2022) leverage both monolingual and multilingual datasets
1834   to perform tasks such as translation and text summarization across a wide spectrum of languages.
1835   However, the predominant focus on English has led to disparities in performance, particularly for
       low-resource languages. Recent work on multilingual LLMs, such as BLOOM (Scao et al., 2022), has

       shown that adapting these English-centric models through vocabulary extension based on multilingual
1872
       corpora and continual pre-training (CPT) can improve performance across languages, especially
1873
       low-resource ones. These models highlight the importance of efficient tokenization and adaptation,
1874   which can bridge the performance gap between high-resource and low-resource languages.
1875
1876
       Multilingual Corpora The availability and use of multilingual corpora play a crucial role in training
1877
       multilingual LLMs. CC100 Corpus (Conneau et al., 2020), launched in 2020, encompasses hundreds
1878
       of billions of tokens and over 100 languages. Further, CC100-XL Corpus (Lin et al., 2022), created
1879   for the training of XGLM, extends across 68 Common Crawl Snapshots and 134 languages, aiming to
1880   balance language presentation and improve performance in few-shot and zero-shot tasks. The ROOTS
1881   Corpus (Laurençon et al., 2022), released in July 2022, supports BLOOM with approximately 341
1882   billion tokens across 46 natural languages. It emphasizes underrepresented languages such as Swahili
1883   and Catalan, drawing from diverse sources including web crawls, books and academic publications.
1884   Besides, Occiglot Fineweb 47 , which began to be released in early 2024, consists of around 230
1885   million documents in 10 European languages. It combines curated and cleaned web data to support
1886   efficient training for both high- and low-resource European languages. Additionally, recent efforts
1887
       parallel corpus construction from web crawls, such as ParaCrawl (Bañón et al., 2020) and CCMatrix
       (Schwenk et al., 2021b), have contributed to large-scale multilingual training too.
1888
1889
            47
                 https://occiglot.eu/posts/occiglot-fineweb/

1938   Continual Pre-training Continual pre-training has become a popular strategy to adapt LLMs
1939   to new languages and domains without retraining from scratch. The process involves updating
1940   model parameters incrementally using a relatively small amount of new data from target languages.
1941   Recent studies, such as those by Tejaswi et al. (2024), have demonstrated the effectiveness of CPT in
1942   improving the adaptability of LLMs in low-resource language settings. Techniques such as vocabulary
1943   augmentation and targeted domain-specific pre-training have been shown to significantly improve
       both efficiency and performance, particularly when large multilingual models are adapted to new

1984
1985   languages. Despite its benefits, CPT can lead to catastrophic forgetting, where models lose previously
1986   learned information. To address the potential degraded performance issue, Ibrahim et al. (2024)
1987   presents a simple yet effective approach to continual pre-train models, demonstrating that with a
1988   combination of learning rate re-warming, re-decaying, and replay of previous data, it is possible
1989   to match the performance of fully re-trained models. Also, recent studies have delved into the
1990   effectiveness of continual pre-training with parallel data. As highlighted in the study by Gilabert
1991   et al. (2024), the use of a Catalan-centric parallel dataset has enabled the training of models good at
1992
       trasnalting in various directions. Besides, the research by Kondo et al. (2024), proposed a two-phase
       continual training approach with parallel data. In the first phase, a pre-trained LLM is continually
1993
       pre-trained on parallel data, followed by a second phase of supervised fine-tuning with a small amount
1994
       of high-quality parallel data. Their experiments with a 3.8B-parameter model across various data
1995   formats revealed that alternating between source and target sentences during continual pre-training is
1996   crucial for enhancing translation accuracy in the corresponding direction.
